<div id="review_2600188225" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2600188225" />
    <a title="anna (½ of readsrainbow)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow"><img alt="anna (½ of readsrainbow)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538064747p2/8645302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2600188225?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="anna (½ of readsrainbow)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="anna (½ of readsrainbow)" href="/user/show/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow">anna (½ of readsrainbow)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=1-contemporary">1-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-lgbt-authors">2-lgbt-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-authors-of-color">2-authors-of-color</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-mlm">2-mlm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-protags-of-color">2-protags-of-color</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=wishlist">wishlist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-ownvoices">2-ownvoices</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=arcs">arcs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=pub-2019">pub-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8645302-anna-of-readsrainbow?shelf=2-lgbt">2-lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2600188225" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1909039968144088912"><b>rep:</b> Vietnamese-American gay mc, Vietnamese characters, gay characters<br/><b>tw:</b> on page death, child abuse, drugs, war descriptions, homophobia, animal violence <br/><br/><b>Review also on my <a href='https://readingpeaches.wordpress.com/2019/05/29/arc-review-on-earth-were-briefly-gorgeous-by-ocean-vuong/' rel='nofollow noopener'>blog</a>. <i>ARC provided by the publisher.</i></b><br/><br/>Ocean Vuong is first & foremost a poet and <em>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</em> is his literary debut (he also has a poem by the same title). It’s not a <em>novel</em> novel, though, not in this very Western sense we’re all used to. There are no prominent arcs or villains, or any ascending tension. It’s </span>
  <span id="freeText1909039968144088912" style="display:none"><b>rep:</b> Vietnamese-American gay mc, Vietnamese characters, gay characters<br /><b>tw:</b> on page death, child abuse, drugs, war descriptions, homophobia, animal violence <br /><br /><b>Review also on my <a href="https://readingpeaches.wordpress.com/2019/05/29/arc-review-on-earth-were-briefly-gorgeous-by-ocean-vuong/" rel="nofollow noopener">blog</a>. <i>ARC provided by the publisher.</i></b><br /><br />Ocean Vuong is first &amp; foremost a poet and <em>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</em> is his literary debut (he also has a poem by the same title). It’s not a <em>novel</em> novel, though, not in this very Western sense we’re all used to. There are no prominent arcs or villains, or any ascending tension. It’s described as a letter from a son to his mother &amp; that’s how it reads, but you could also call it a memoir and not be too far off the mark.<br /><br />The book is divided intro three sections, none of them with titles, apart from simple Roman numbers. But their themes are obvious nonetheless (being an immigrant in the US, being gay, dying) and they're overflowing with emotions. You can’t really forget that Vuong is a poet, with how beautifully crafted this novel is. He doesn’t often name things, instead lets himself be vague with metaphors &amp; trusts the reader will understand what he’s getting at anyway. The whole experience is a lot like reading a poem, but this isn’t just a novel in verse &amp; it’s not just a letter, either. It blurs the lines and it does it without you even noticing.<br /><br />Just like everything Vuong published so far, <em>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</em> is very raw, visceral even and vicious at times. But all the feelings (pain) it evokes ring true. That’s the real strength of Vuong’s novel: the honesty evident not only in the emotions it brings to life, but in the life itself that it describes; all the ups-and-downs, all the ugly details, all the not-poetry-like details. There’s no shying away from the mundanity of life here, from parts the fairy tales (and porn) omit.<br /><br /><br /><br /><a href="https://readingpeaches.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">

  <b>Bloglovin</b>
</a> | <a href="https://readsrainbow.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2623769467" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2623769467" />
    <a title="Marchpane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6677659-marchpane"><img alt="Marchpane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483537315p2/6677659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2623769467?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marchpane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marchpane" href="/user/show/6677659-marchpane">Marchpane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42111754-on-earth-we-re-briefly-gorgeous">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6677659-marchpane?shelf=2019-releases">2019-releases</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2623769467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4445251631776063179">Books by poets always have the best titles, don’t they? <i>
  <b>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</b>
</i>, acclaimed poet Ocean Vuong’s semi-autobiographical debut novel, takes the form of a letter from Little Dog to his mother, both Vietnamese refugees now living in the U.S. This poignant, lyrical story more than delivers on the promise of that striking title. <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“Because gunshots, lies, and oxtail—or whatever you want to call your god—should say Yes over and over, in cycles, in spirals, with no other reason</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4445251631776063179" style="display:none">Books by poets always have the best titles, don’t they? <i>
  <b>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</b>
</i>, acclaimed poet Ocean Vuong’s semi-autobiographical debut novel, takes the form of a letter from Little Dog to his mother, both Vietnamese refugees now living in the U.S. This poignant, lyrical story more than delivers on the promise of that striking title. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Because gunshots, lies, and oxtail—or whatever you want to call your god—should say Yes over and over, in cycles, in spirals, with no other reason but to hear itself exist. Because love, at its best, repeats itself. Shouldn’t it?”</i>
</blockquote><br />It is an emotionally affecting tale wrought with a poet’s artistry. Little Dog translating for his mother on their awkward outings together; his grandmother’s experiences during the war in Vietnam (<i>“I did what any mother would do, I made a way to eat. Who can judge me, huh? Who?”</i>); Little Dog’s first love and adolescent fumblings with farm boy Trevor. I was glued to the page, though it isn’t what you would normally call a page-turner. Violence and trauma permeate throughout, and this does not make for an ‘easy’ read, yet I could not put it down. There are also many tender moments, and even humour too.<br /><br />Little Dog’s relationship with his mother is not the sole focus – rather this is a coming-of-age (and coming-out) story, woven with family history. Little Dog’s knowledge that his mother, with her limited education &amp; grasp of English, will never read his words frees up the narrative and allows Vuong to deviate from the second-person voice when the story requires it. <br /><br />The many moving and memorable scenes don’t quite cohere the way a more traditional novel would. Figurative language abounds, much of it stunning, but the odd passage jarred and jolted me out of the narrative (<i>“Then his eyelashes. You could hear them think”</i>). Vuong’s portrait of Little Dog, Hong and Lan is overlaid with a filigree of intricate prose: the effect is pretty, but it makes it difficult to see them clearly, a fact made more frustrating by the utterly compelling nature of their stories.<br /><br />Some essayistic digressions are slightly incongruous. The first, about Tiger Woods’ family history, draws out connections to Little Dog’s story, exploring racial identity in the American context, and Woods’ father’s involvement in the Vietnam war. The second, a series of factoids about Purdue Pharma, provides background to the opioid crisis that devastates Little Dog’s social circle, but its insertion feels unnecessary: this is information most readers will already know.<br /><br /><i>On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous</i> is a novel that asserts itself defiantly – to take exception with its many idiosyncrasies feels churlish. As Vuong plays with language and syntax in order to blend poetic and novelistic techniques, he blurs the boundaries between the two forms. Someone who reads more poetry than I do would surely find it easier to embrace. As for myself, I found it to be frequently, if somewhat inconsistently, gorgeous. 4 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="4445251631776063179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2812200010" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2812200010" />
    <a title="Henk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76202320-henk"><img alt="Henk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557871304p2/76202320.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2812200010?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henk" href="/user/show/76202320-henk">Henk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781787331501" href="/book/show/41955384-on-earth-we-re-briefly-gorgeous">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76202320-henk?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2812200010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8383172803329390507"><b>Beautiful and poetic, a meditation on growing up in rural, dying America as a gay immigrant</b><br/><i>That time at the Chinese butcher, you pointed to the roasted pig hanging from its hook. “The ribs are just like q person’s after they’re burned.” You let out a clipped chuckle, then paused, rook out your pocketbook, your face pinched, and recounted our money.</i><br/><br/><b>Mother and Son</b><br/><i>When does a war end? When can I say your name and have it mean only your name and not what you left behind?</i><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41880609.On_Earth_We_re_Briefly_Gorgeous' title='On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous by Ocean Vuong' rel='nofollow noopener'>On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeo</a></span>
  <span id="freeText8383172803329390507" style="display:none"><b>Beautiful and poetic, a meditation on growing up in rural, dying America as a gay immigrant</b><br /><i>That time at the Chinese butcher, you pointed to the roasted pig hanging from its hook. “The ribs are just like q person’s after they’re burned.” You let out a clipped chuckle, then paused, rook out your pocketbook, your face pinched, and recounted our money.</i><br /><br /><b>Mother and Son</b><br /><i>When does a war end? When can I say your name and have it mean only your name and not what you left behind?</i><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41880609.On_Earth_We_re_Briefly_Gorgeous" title="On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous by Ocean Vuong" rel="nofollow noopener">On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous</a> starts of with a lot of supressed violence from Little Dog’s mom, channeled towards him while growing up in Hartford, Connecticut. <br />Little Dog as narrator tells the poignant story of him growing up as the son of an immigrant Vietnamese, by writing a letter to his illiterate mother. His name is poetically explained: (<i>To love something, then, is to name it after something so worthless it might be left untouched - and alive</i>) and the poetic title of the book is only further augmented by the language, that is lush and full of beautiful images and metaphors. This while the underlying story is quite brutal, encompassing not just the perils and sacrifices of his mother, but also the dangers of growing up in rural America in the midst of an opioid epidemic.<br /><br />Little Dog acting as his mother and grandmother’s interpreter, culminating in a scene where he explains as an eight year old that he was her child, born from her asshole to a store assistent at Sears, since there is a taboo to talk about the genitalia in Vietnamese, is both saddening as sometimes hilarious.<br /><br />An other scene I admired was the depiction of how the lack of language frustrates his mom, when Little Dog tells about being bullied, and she hits him, before coming up with the solution he needs to become bigger and she starts to feed him gallons of milk each and every morning. <br /><br />There is so much deep emotions tied to these sections of the book, to the life stories of his mother and grandmother, and so many beautiful sentences on how Little Dog navigates the world and reflects on historical violence:<br /><br /><i>I touch the world not as myself but as an echo of who I was.<br /><br />Memory is a choice. You said that once, with your back to me, the way a god would say it.<br /><br />Because a bullet without a body is a song without ears.<br /><br />She examined her hand, as if to make sure it still existed, then dropped it.<br /><br />“Remember”, you said each morning before we stepped out in cold Connecticut air, “don’t draw attention to yourself. You’re already Vietnamese.”</i><br /><br /><b>Son and lover</b><br /><i>I studied him like a new word.</i><br /><br />In a certain way I feel the book could have revolved solely around the family relations without any problem, but <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4456871.Ocean_Vuong" title="Ocean Vuong" rel="nofollow noopener">Ocean Vuong</a> also weaves in a touching love story. His relationship with Trevor, a hillbilly, redneck, drugs doing first boyfriend, feels like a train crash you see happening in slow motion. Actually the coming out to his mother doesn't seem to go very disasterous, but show the unbridgeable gap between his mothers language and his own world when there is no fitting, non-colonial word that doesn’t refer to pedophilia to describe homosexuality in Vietnamese.<br /><br />His reflections on his first relationship, and the power dynamics in this (with Trevor asserting that this is only a phase for him, but something forever for Little Dog, also tied to who penetrates who) are very well done in my opinion. We are offered phrases as: <i>Sometimes being offered tenderness feels like the very proof that you’ve been ruined.</i><br />And genuine tenderness comes back for instances in the depiction of the first time the boys have sex with each other.<br /><br />In the end the power of class in modern day America, with overdoses and immigrants barely scraping a living together, opioid epidemics and derelict towns with trailer parks, take precedent. <br />But the elegy to a first love is beautiful, nonetheless: <i>We were exchanging truths, I realized, which is to say, we were cutting one another. &amp; You wore a face.</i><br /><br />Intimacy (mostly physical and sometimes tender with his first lover while having sex) and a lack of possibility of being truly intimate with the one’s closest to you (like how he longs for being a word and his whole being, laid down besides his mother, just to convene everything he is) is also beautifully tackled by Ocean Vuong.<br /><br />I was immersed into On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous, and found it both convincing and emotionally impactful. The language is sometimes too flowery one could say, but Vuong offers us gems of sentences and I feel he is a very great literary talent who captures both the power of stories and loss:<br /><i>...where to be lost is never to be wrong, but simply more.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8383172803329390507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2810183394" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2810183394" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2810183394?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=immigration">immigration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=race">race</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=sexuality">sexuality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=totally-unexpected">totally-unexpected</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=america">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2810183394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7582585074015354505">This work is called a novel but it is a ball of flame tossed into a dark night, blinding, brilliant, searing. Who knows if it is poetry or novel or memoir; the language fills the mouth and is saturated with truth. We recognize it. We’ve tasted it. We are pained by it. It still hurts.<br/><br/>Something here is reminiscent of the epic poetry of Homer. Life's brutality, man’s frailty, the odyssey, the clash of civilizations, the incomparable language undeniably capturing human experience, these things make </span>
  <span id="freeText7582585074015354505" style="display:none">This work is called a novel but it is a ball of flame tossed into a dark night, blinding, brilliant, searing. Who knows if it is poetry or novel or memoir; the language fills the mouth and is saturated with truth. We recognize it. We’ve tasted it. We are pained by it. It still hurts.<br /><br />Something here is reminiscent of the epic poetry of Homer. Life's brutality, man’s frailty, the odyssey, the clash of civilizations, the incomparable language undeniably capturing human experience, these things make Vuong someone who heightens our awareness, deepens our experience, shocks us into acknowledgement of our shared experiences. What have we in common with a Greek of ancient times singing of a war and the personal trials of man? What have we in common with a gay immigrant boy writing of war and the personal trials of man?<br /><br />The story is clear enough but fragmentary. In a Nov 2017 <a href="https://lithub.com/ocean-vuong-interrogating-the-canon-and-literally-riding-a-bicycle-with-no-hands/" rel="nofollow noopener">LitHub interview</a>, Vuong tells us <blockquote>”I’m writing a novel composed of woven inter-genre fragments. To me, a book made entirely out of unbridged fractures feels most faithful to the physical and psychological displacement I experience as a human being. I’m interested in a novel that consciously rejects the notion that something has to be whole in order to tell a complete story. I also want to interrogate the arbitrary measurements of a “successful” literary work, particularly as it relates to canonical Western values. For example, we traditionally privilege congruency and balance in fiction, we want our themes linked, our conflicts “resolved,” and our plots “ironed out.” But when one arrives at the page through colonized, plundered, and erased histories and diasporas, to write a smooth and cohesive novel is to ultimately write a lie. In a way, I’m curious about a work that rejects its patriarchal predecessors as a way of accepting its fissured self. I think, perennially, of Theresa Hak Kyung Cha’s Dictée. This resistance to dominant convention is not only the isolated concern of marginalized writers—but all writers—and perhaps especially white writers, who can gain so much by questioning how the ways we value art can replicate the very oppressive legacies we strive to end.”</blockquote>The novel he speaks of is this one. I did not understand that paragraph when I first read it as well as I do now. I am more aware, too, having looked closely for the Western world’s acknowledged historical tendency to erase or ignore pieces of experience not congruent with their own worldview.<br /><br />The language Vuong brings is exquisite and extraordinary: “The fluorescent hums steady above them, as if the scene is a dream the light is having.” “…the thing about beauty is that it’s only beautiful outside of itself.” “The carpet under his bare feet is shiny as spilled oil from years of wear.” “…repeating piles of rotted firewood, the oily mounds gone mushy…” “He had a thick face and pomaded hair, even at this hour, like Elvis on on his last day on earth.”<br /><br />Vuong repeats motifs to tie the experiences of one person to the rest of his life, to tie one person’s experiences to those of others: “I’m at war.” “We cracked up. We cracked open.” “…you never see yourself if you’re the sun. You don’t even know where you are in the sky.” “…my cheek bone stinging from the first blow.” “I was yellow.”<br /><br />A teen, immigrated to the U.S. from Vietnam with his mother, grandmother, and aunt finds himself fleeing his “shitty high school to spend [his] days in New York lost in library stacks,” from whence he, first in this family to go to college, squanders his opportunity on an English degree. <br /><br />The teen discovers his gayness and does not flee it, though his white lover agonizes and denies all his life. We watch that boy fall, wither, die under the scourge of fentanyl and opioid addiction and Vuong places the scourge in the wider context of an awry world. <br /><br />Despite (or perhaps because of) the fragmented, shattered nature of the tale, there is a real momentum to this novel, Vuong telling us things not articulated in this way before: a familiar war from a new angle, the friction burn of the immigrant experience, the roughness of gay sex, the madness of living untethered in the world. The language is so precise, so surprising, so wide-awake and fresh, that we read to see. <br /><br />Last year, in September of 2018, I reviewed Vuong’s first book of poetry, <a href="https://thebowedbookshelf.blogspot.com/2018/09/night-sky-with-exit-wounds-by-ocean.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Sky with Exit Wounds</a>. The poems had many of the same tendencies toward epic poetry—they were big, and meaningful. <a href="http://bit.ly/2Lx3n2p" rel="nofollow noopener">On my blog</a> I have attached a short video of Vuong reading from that collection to give you some idea of his power. You're welcome, readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7582585074015354505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2896870638" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2896870638" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2896870638?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2896870638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2873925400497248040">Vuong’s voice is delicate and scathing all at once, his words heal and wound and palpitate with raw intensity, like the heart of a caged animal that knows its end is near and is absorbing everything its brief life has offered, the beautiful and the horrible, leaving nothing out.<br/><br/>This book is a son’s letter to his illiterate mother, a tender and desperate attempt to put down into words what he never said out loud, and probably never will, to the tormented woman who loved and abused him because she</span>
  <span id="freeText2873925400497248040" style="display:none">Vuong’s voice is delicate and scathing all at once, his words heal and wound and palpitate with raw intensity, like the heart of a caged animal that knows its end is near and is absorbing everything its brief life has offered, the beautiful and the horrible, leaving nothing out.<br /><br />This book is a son’s letter to his illiterate mother, a tender and desperate attempt to put down into words what he never said out loud, and probably never will, to the tormented woman who loved and abused him because she had no other way to escape the ghosts of her war-ravaged past. It reads like a memoir, although it seems both characters, mother and son, are fictional. Nevertheless, it’s hard to believe that such intense confidence might be the result of just imagination. The sense of dislocation, of discovering a kind of love that is tainted and condemned by many, the confusion of growing up not knowing whether you are loved or even tolerated by your own family; the beauty of coming to terms with who you are and not minding the consequences, it all sounds so real that the narrator’s voice might easily be confused with the author’s words.<br /><br />Words. Words stop being words as we know them in Vuong’s hands. He molds and combines them in ways that make them entirely free with a renewed meaning of their own, flowing effortlessly like a river does, dragging memories, experiences, yearnings and trauma into its current, making them all comfortable and natural with the tone of Vuong’s storytelling.<br />This book reads like sheer poetry, it’s an impressionistic collage of images and stolen moments that converges onto a profound understanding of the narrator’s evolution, breathing out life and experience and the repression known by those who are rootless and adrift in a society that will never fully embrace them. <br />Vietnamese? American? Gay? Bipolar? What do these words really mean when confronted with the sheer crudity of feelings exposed, skin bared and blood spilled?<br /><br />I fell under the spell of Vuong’s mastery with words and his nimble use of lyrical imagery. Introspection never sounded more excruciatingly exquisite and the concept of family was never exposed so truthfully, both the ugly and the beautiful. <br />I followed Vuong’s word-trail till the end of the cliff, and like the Bison that follows the herd blindly, I didn’t stop and leapt off with him, drawn by his force of nature, not minding where that would lead me; and for a split of a second, I felt briefly gorgeous.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2873925400497248040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2878680362" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2878680362" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2878680362?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Seemita by: </span>
      Twitter
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=epislotary">epislotary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=vietnam">vietnam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=immigration">immigration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=debut">debut</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=me">me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=singing_words">singing_words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=culture">culture</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2878680362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4272248398826771427">Memory. <br/><br/>A twig to hang our old ages on, a current to charge our failed youth from; a blanket to hush our demons from the black, a spring to nourish our wounded dreams underneath. A whole. A hollow. And everything suspended in between is memory.<i>
  <blockquote>'Ma. You once told me that memory is a choice. But if you were god, you’d know it’s a flood.'</blockquote>
</i>And like a flood, Little Dog untethers me from my world and takes me to his’, where he writes a letter and recounts his life of 28 odd years to his Ma, who is </span>
  <span id="freeText4272248398826771427" style="display:none">Memory. <br /><br />A twig to hang our old ages on, a current to charge our failed youth from; a blanket to hush our demons from the black, a spring to nourish our wounded dreams underneath. A whole. A hollow. And everything suspended in between is memory.<i>
  <blockquote>'Ma. You once told me that memory is a choice. But if you were god, you’d know it’s a flood.'</blockquote>
</i>And like a flood, Little Dog untethers me from my world and takes me to his’, where he writes a letter and recounts his life of 28 odd years to his Ma, who is illiterate. Does the secrecy of our identity embolden us to admit truths that we would otherwise have fought tooth and nail to negate? Perhaps. May be that’s why, in an infallibly raw and unfiltered tone, Little Dog talks about his early impressions of his grandma, Lan and his ma, Rose, that coincide with their immigration to America from Vietnam under the shelling quiver of Vietnam War. <br /><br />His letter glows with the holes in the armour of both the women’s defiance in the face of extremely trying circumstances and lowly professions because he proudly plasters his heart with their frailties and failures as it makes them human, and like him.<i>
  <blockquote>'A fake. A fraud. Which was why I loved it.'</blockquote>
</i>His letter trembles with the bittersweet sensation of making love the first time at an age where his gay identity was as much a stranger to him as it was to the world outside, and the rude realization that even under wraps, Love was a prisoner of societal rules. <i>
  <blockquote>'I got what I wanted—a boy swimming toward me. Except I was no shore, Ma. I was driftwood trying to remember what I had broken from to get here.'</blockquote>
</i>His letter forgives the unspoken words of his grandfather and the truant tendencies of his ma. His letter accommodates the big questions of life hovering on dignity, fairness and humanity, without losing sight of the small joys of a flight of monarchs and content sleep of his ma after her son’s kneading. His letter brushes the roots of Saigon and the skies of Connecticut. His letter turns resplendent under the powerful lamp of story-telling, the kind that undulates on the elated and visceral veins of family, friendship, love and life.<i>
  <blockquote>'Do you remember the happiest day of your life? What about the saddest? Do you ever wonder if sadness and happiness can be combined, to make a deep purple feeling, not good, not bad, but remarkable simply because you didn’t have to live on one side or the other?'</blockquote>
</i>Air.<br /><br />An invisible shield that keeps us breathing. An indefinable nothingness that is necessary. Sometimes, the bearer of freedom and sometimes, the pall of gloom. But every time, there. Always. Inevitable. Urgent. And like air is this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4272248398826771427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13344804-boustrophedon?shelf=united-states">united-states</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2846015068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12728684090336708137">I gave Vuong’s full-length poetry debut 1 star, which made a lot of people upset in the same way as when you tell a high school student that their favorite band sucks. Now I wish I had given it 2 stars, because this book is worse. Worse still is what Vuong represents: the literary establishment unchanged despite our being told otherwise. No, it has not opened itself to new voices; yes, it is still as capital-driven as ever. The only new, marginalized voices it chooses are those that will sell. V</span>
  <span id="freeText12728684090336708137" style="display:none">I gave Vuong’s full-length poetry debut 1 star, which made a lot of people upset in the same way as when you tell a high school student that their favorite band sucks. Now I wish I had given it 2 stars, because this book is worse. Worse still is what Vuong represents: the literary establishment unchanged despite our being told otherwise. No, it has not opened itself to new voices; yes, it is still as capital-driven as ever. The only new, marginalized voices it chooses are those that will sell. Vuong sells—he writes like Rupi Kaur doing an MFA—with a salable backstory.<br /><br />A recent New Yorker “review” of the book illustrates what I mean. The majority of the article is a summation of the book’s plot. There are the usual words written about Vuong’s personal story that change each retelling (he learned to read when he was 16, he learned to read when he was 14, he never learned to read but writes by telepathy, etc.). Vuong, the spoiled only-child of the literary world, is praised for the most basic of thoughts, a round of applause every time he poops. We are told he is great at wordplay, but then are dished this: “It’s not fair that the word laughter is trapped inside slaughter” as an example—a joke so old that I’ve literally seen similar sentiments printed on t-shirts (seriously, Google “laughter spell slaughter” and you get mugs, hats, and other items for sale). But there are no better examples to choose from in the book. I searched.<br /><br />Am I being harsh? How about: to pay lip service to an establishment figure is to prop up the establishment. I am saddened to think of all the POC and queer (and yes, white and straight) young writers whose voices I will not hear because those voices are their own and in being their own are more difficult and therefore less marketable, while being told—with Vuong trotted out as an example—that the literary world is seeking those voices. Strangely enough, for a book that parallels its author’s life closely and seems so personal, I hardly hear Vuong at all. What I hear instead is a writer given too much praise too young and who will now always write with that imp on his shoulder, which may be why the prose aims for freshness but, like a pillow whose two sides have already been slept on, now feels lukewarm and molded to someone else’s head (and lumpy, there is nothing smooth about his sentences; they do not come naturally). He writes like he thinks a great writer should write without being a great writer himself: each line is so overthought, so straining to be something great, trying so hard to be profound:<br /><br />“You once told me that the human eye is god’s loneliest creation. How so much of the world passes through the pupil and still it holds nothing. The eye, alone in its socket, doesn’t even known there’s another one, just like it, an inch away, just as hungry, as empty.”<br /><br />This is prose so purple it is supra-spectral. After a few pages you want to stop and read something less pretentious. I did and I did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12728684090336708137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2859613482" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2859613482" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2859613482?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780525562030" href="/book/show/39866294-on-earth-we-re-briefly-gorgeous">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=asia2019">asia2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-vietnam">location-vietnam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2019">read2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=reviewcopy">reviewcopy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-usa-connecticut">location-usa-connecticut</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2859613482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7117379596987216531">I knew this book would have an emotional wallop so I held off for a while. It's clear Ocean Vuong is drawing on his own experience in this novel, because it shares some of the sentiments and emotions I experienced in his poetry collection, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23841432.Night_Sky_with_Exit_Wounds' title='Night Sky with Exit Wounds by Ocean Vuong' rel='nofollow noopener'>Night Sky with Exit Wounds</a>.<br/><br/>Oh how I love when a poet writes essays or novels. The language is powerful, the way some pieces are linear but others return to themes and core experiences is very moving. It starts out speaking to the violence in families, looks at</span>
  <span id="freeText7117379596987216531" style="display:none">I knew this book would have an emotional wallop so I held off for a while. It's clear Ocean Vuong is drawing on his own experience in this novel, because it shares some of the sentiments and emotions I experienced in his poetry collection, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23841432.Night_Sky_with_Exit_Wounds" title="Night Sky with Exit Wounds by Ocean Vuong" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Sky with Exit Wounds</a>.<br /><br />Oh how I love when a poet writes essays or novels. The language is powerful, the way some pieces are linear but others return to themes and core experiences is very moving. It starts out speaking to the violence in families, looks at language and belonging, moves to sexuality and connection (even when the other person is flawed,) all through the narration of "Little Dog" in a letter to his mama, one he doesn't believe she will ever read, so he can be honest. <br /><br />I find some parallels with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23168277.The_Sympathizer" title="The Sympathizer by Viet Thanh Nguyen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sympathizer</a> by Viet Thanh Nguyen, in the sense that they both used fiction to explore their experiences leaving Vietnam and coming of age in America, but Nguyen's exploration is topical and political while Vuong explores the heart and mind. Both look at memory but the two novels are so very different.<br /><br />The WaPo review by Ron Charles pointed me to the <a href="https://soundcloud.com/penguin-audio/on-earth-were-briefly-gorgeous" rel="nofollow noopener">audio excerpt in SoundCloud read by the author</a>. If your heart can take it, the audio sounds powerful, but I would have had to take even more time to listen because I find the emotional intensity a bit overwhelming. But, you know, more of that please.<br /><br /><i>I had a copy of this novel from the publisher through NetGalley. It came out June 4, 2019.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7117379596987216531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('D+HZ8b1f2Dkvs0yioCFX4bAcHhcnEhB3mLcqwZIxX3oZqPkcNg0SW2IEZhtIA9VGHLrWS3zXDnn3if8WluOAQw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jTUHCbAL/AqEiF/BEB+Ttt/DJYuR+uUoagxjZAqvq7GbfCfkO1k2aMk/dXj4PRERc2Xt18o/+yYFMrazDn10iA==')})
  }
//]]>

Elwood’s only salvation is his friendship with fellow “delinquent” Turner, which deepens despite Turner’s conviction that Elwood is hopelessly naive, that the world is crooked, and that the only way to survive is to scheme and avoid trouble. As life at the Academy becomes ever more perilous, the tension between Elwood’s ideals and Turner’s skepticism leads to a decision whose repercussions will echo down the decades.\n \nBased on the real story of a reform school that operated for 111 years and warped the lives of thousands of children, The Nickel Boys is a devastating, driven narrative that showcases a great American novelist writing Each piece builds into a larger examination of race and queerness, power and vulnerability, love and grief: a portrait of what we all do for one another—and to one another—as we fight to become ourselves.Blending poetry and prose, Jones has developed a style that is equal parts sensual, beautiful, and powerful—a voice that’s by turns a river, a blues, and a nightscape set ablaze.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4456871.Ocean_Vuong">About Ocean Vuong</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4456871.Ocean_Vuong"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1561472666p3/4456871.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4456871.Ocean_Vuong">Ocean Vuong

3,055

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18364425628320814268">Ocean Vuong is the author of the debut novel, <em>On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous</em>, out from Penguin Press (2019) and forthcoming in 15 other languages worldwide. He is also the author of the critically acclaimed poetry collection, <em>Night Sky with Exit Wounds</em>, a <em>New York Times</em> Top 10 Book of 2016, winner of the T.S. Eliot Prize, the Whiting Award, the Thom Gunn Award, and the Forward Prize for Best Firs</span>
  <span id="freeText18364425628320814268" style="display:none">Ocean Vuong is the author of the debut novel, <em>On Earth We're Briefly Gorgeous</em>, out from Penguin Press (2019) and forthcoming in 15 other languages worldwide. He is also the author of the critically acclaimed poetry collection, <em>Night Sky with Exit Wounds</em>, a <em>New York Times</em> Top 10 Book of 2016, winner of the T.S. Eliot Prize, the Whiting Award, the Thom Gunn Award, and the Forward Prize for Best First Collection. A Ruth Lilly fellow from the Poetry Foundation, his honors include fellowships from the Lannan Foundation, the Civitella Ranieri Foundation, The Elizabeth George Foundation, The Academy of American Poets, and the Pushcart Prize.<br /> <br />Vuong's writings have been featured in <em>The Atlantic</em>, <em>Harpers</em>, <em>The Nation</em>, <em>New Republic</em>, <em>The New Yorker</em>, <em>The New York Times</em>, <em>The Village Voice</em>, and <em>American Poetry Review</em>, which awarded him the Stanley Kunitz Prize for Younger Poets. Selected by Foreign Policy magazine as a 2016 100 Leading Global Thinker, alongside Hillary Clinton, Ban Ki-Moon and Justin Trudeau, Ocean was also named by BuzzFeed Books as one of “32 Essential Asian American Writers” and has been profiled on NPR’s “All Things Considered,” PBS NewsHour, <em>Teen Vogue</em>, <em>VICE</em>, <em>The Fantastic Man</em>, and <em>The New Yorker</em>.<br /><br />Born in Saigon, Vietnam, he lives in Northampton, Massachusetts, where he serves as an Assistant Professor in the MFA Program for Poets and Writers at Umass-Amherst. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18364425628320814268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        script.src = load[i];
        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);
      }
    }
  if (w.attachEvent) {
    w.attachEvent('onload', go);
  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
  }
}(window, document,
    ['//assets.pinterest.com/js/pinit.js',
     '//apis.google.com/js/plusone.js'
    ]
  ));

//]]>
</script>

    </div>
